During the <a href="/wiki/Greek_War_of_Independence" title="Greek War of Independence">Greek War of Independence</a>, the combined navies of <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">Britain</a>, France and <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a> defeated an <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman</a> fleet at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Navarino" title="Battle of Navarino">Battle of Navarino</a> in 1827, the last major action between sailing ships. During the same period, the Royal Navy took <a href="/wiki/Piracy" title="Piracy">anti-piracy</a> actions in the <a href="/wiki/South_China_Sea" title="South China Sea">South China Sea</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> Between 1807 and 1865, it maintained a <a href="/wiki/Blockade_of_Africa" title="Blockade of Africa">Blockade of Africa</a> to counter the illegal <a href="/wiki/Slave_trade" class="mw-redirect" title="Slave trade">slave trade</a>. It also participated in the <a href="/wiki/Crimean_War" title="Crimean War">Crimean War</a> of 1854\xe2\x80\x9356, as well as numerous military actions throughout Asia and Africa, notably the <a href="/wiki/First_Opium_War" title="First Opium War">First</a> and <a href="/wiki/Second_Opium_War" title="Second Opium War">Second Opium Wars</a> with <a href="/wiki/Qing_dynasty" title="Qing dynasty">Qing dynasty</a> China. On 27 August 1896, the Royal Navy took part in the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Zanzibar_War" title="Anglo-Zanzibar War">Anglo-Zanzibar War</a>, which was the shortest war in history.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The end of the 19th century saw structural changes brought about by the <a href="/wiki/First_Sea_Lord" title="First Sea Lord">First Sea Lord</a> <a href="/wiki/John_Fisher,_1st_Baron_Fisher" title="John Fisher, 1st Baron Fisher">Jackie Fisher</a>, who retired, scrapped or placed into reserve many of the older vessels, making funds and manpower available for newer ships. He also oversaw the development of <a href="/wiki/HMS_Dreadnought_(1906)" title="HMS Dreadnought (1906)">HMS&#160;<i>Dreadnought</i></a>, launched in 1906. Its speed and firepower rendered all existing battleships obsolete. The industrial and economic development of <a href="/wiki/German_Empire" title="German Empire">Germany</a> had by this time overtaken Britain, enabling the <a href="/wiki/Imperial_German_Navy" title="Imperial German Navy">Imperial German Navy</a> to attempt to outpace British construction of <a href="/wiki/Dreadnoughts" class="mw-redirect" title="Dreadnoughts">dreadnoughts</a>. In the ensuing <a href="/wiki/Anglo-German_naval_arms_race" title="Anglo-German naval arms race">arms race</a>, Britain succeeded in maintaining a substantial numerical advantage over Germany, but for the first time since 1805 another navy now existed with the capacity to challenge the Royal Navy in battle.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Reforms were also gradually introduced in the conditions for enlisted men with the abolition of military <a href="/wiki/Flogging" class="mw-redirect" title="Flogging">flogging</a> in 1879, amongst others.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:HMS_Dreadnought_1906_H61017.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/HMS_Dreadnought_1906_H61017.jpg/220px-HMS_Dreadnought_1906_H61017.jpg" width="220" height="166" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/HMS_Dreadnought_1906_H61017.jpg/330px-HMS_Dreadnought_1906_H61017.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/HMS_Dreadnought_1906_H61017.jpg/440px-HMS_Dreadnought_1906_H61017.jpg 2x" data-file-width="740" data-file-height="558" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:HMS_Dreadnought_1906_H61017.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/HMS_Dreadnought_(1906)" title="HMS Dreadnought (1906)">HMS&#160;<i>Dreadnought</i></a></div></div></div>\n<h4><span id="1914.E2.80.931939"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1914\xe2\x80\x931939">1914\xe2\x80\x931939</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Royal_Navy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: 1914\xe2\x80\x931939">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>During the <a href="/wiki/First_World_War" class="mw-redirect" title="First World War">First World War</a>, the Royal Navy\'s strength was mostly deployed at home in the <a href="/wiki/British_Grand_Fleet" class="mw-redirect" title="British Grand Fleet">Grand Fleet</a>, confronting the German <a href="/wiki/High_Seas_Fleet" title="High Seas Fleet">High Seas Fleet</a> across the North Sea. Several inconclusive clashes took place between them, chiefly the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Jutland" title="Battle of Jutland">Battle of Jutland</a> in 1916. The British numerical advantage proved insurmountable, leading the High Seas Fleet to abandon any attempt to challenge British dominance.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Elsewhere in the world, the Navy hunted down the handful of German surface raiders at large. During the <a href="/wiki/Dardanelles_Campaign" class="mw-redirect" title="Dardanelles Campaign">Dardanelles Campaign</a> against the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a> in 1915, it suffered heavy losses during a failed attempt to break through the system of minefields and shore batteries defending the straits.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Upon entering the war, the Navy had immediately established a <a href="/wiki/Blockade_of_Germany" title="Blockade of Germany">blockade of Germany</a>. The Navy\'s <a href="/wiki/Northern_Patrol" title="Northern Patrol">Northern Patrol</a> closed off access to the <a href="/wiki/North_Sea" title="North Sea">North Sea</a>, while the <a href="/wiki/Dover_Patrol" title="Dover Patrol">Dover Patrol</a> closed off access to the <a href="/wiki/English_Channel" title="English Channel">English Channel</a>. The Navy also mined the North Sea. As well as closing off the Imperial German Navy\'s access to the Atlantic, the blockade largely blocked neutral merchant shipping heading to or from <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Germany</a>. The blockade was maintained during the eight months after the armistice was agreed to force Germany to end the war and sign the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Versailles" title="Treaty of Versailles">Treaty of Versailles</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The most serious menace faced by the Navy came from the attacks on merchant shipping mounted by German <a href="/wiki/U-boats" class="mw-redirect" title="U-boats">U-boats</a>. For much of the war this submarine campaign was restricted by <a href="/wiki/Prize_rules" class="mw-redirect" title="Prize rules">prize rules</a> requiring merchant ships to be warned and evacuated before sinking. In 1915, the Germans renounced these restrictions and began to sink merchant ships on sight, but later returned to the previous <a href="/wiki/Rules_of_engagement" title="Rules of engagement">rules of engagement</a> to placate neutral opinion. A resumption of unrestricted submarine warfare in 1917 raised the prospect of Britain and its allies being starved into submission. The Navy\'s response to this new form of warfare had proved inadequate due to its refusal to adopt a <a href="/wiki/Convoy" title="Convoy">convoy</a> system for merchant shipping, despite the demonstrated effectiveness of the technique in protecting troop ships. The belated introduction of convoys sharply reduced losses and brought the U-boat threat under control.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:HMS_Ark_Royal_h85716.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/HMS_Ark_Royal_h85716.jpg/220px-HMS_Ark_Royal_h85716.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/HMS_Ark_Royal_h85716.jpg/330px-HMS_Ark_Royal_h85716.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/HMS_Ark_Royal_h85716.jpg/440px-HMS_Ark_Royal_h85716.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3200" data-file-height="2130" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:HMS_Ark_Royal_h85716.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/HMS_Ark_Royal_(91)" title="HMS Ark Royal (91)">HMS&#160;<i>Ark Royal</i></a></div></div></div>\n<p>In the inter-war period, the Royal Navy was stripped of much of its power. The <a href="/wiki/Washington_Naval_Treaty" title="Washington Naval Treaty">Washington</a> and <a href="/wiki/London_Naval_Treaty" title="London Naval Treaty">London</a> Naval Treaties imposed the scrapping of some capital ships and limitations on new construction.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> In 1932, the <a href="/wiki/Invergordon_Mutiny" title="Invergordon Mutiny">Invergordon Mutiny</a> took place over a proposed 25% pay cut, which was eventually reduced to 10%.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> International tensions increased in the mid-1930s and the <a href="/wiki/Second_London_Naval_Treaty" title="Second London Naval Treaty">Second London Naval Treaty</a> of 1935 failed to halt the development of a naval <a href="/wiki/Arms_race" title="Arms race">arms race</a>. By 1938, treaty limits were effectively being ignored. The re-armament of the Royal Navy was well under way by this point; the Royal Navy had begun construction of the still treaty-affected and undergunned <a href="/wiki/King_George_V-class_battleship_(1939)" title="King George V-class battleship (1939)">new battleships</a> and its first full-sized purpose-built aircraft carriers. In addition to new construction, several existing old battleships (whose gun power offset to a significant extent the weakly armed new battleships), battlecruisers and heavy cruisers were reconstructed, and anti-aircraft weaponry reinforced, while new technologies, such as <a href="/wiki/ASDIC" class="mw-redirect" title="ASDIC">ASDIC</a>, <a href="/wiki/Huff-Duff" class="mw-redirect" title="Huff-Duff">Huff-Duff</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hydrophones" class="mw-redirect" title="Hydrophones">hydrophones</a>, were developed. The Navy had lost control of naval aviation when the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Naval_Air_Service" title="Royal Naval Air Service">Royal Naval Air Service</a> was merged with the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Flying_Corps" title="Royal Flying Corps">Royal Flying Corps</a> to form the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Air_Force" title="Royal Air Force">Royal Air Force</a> in 1918, but regained control of ship-board aircraft with the return of the <a href="/wiki/Fleet_Air_Arm" title="Fleet Air Arm">Fleet Air Arm</a> to Naval control in 1937.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span id="1939.E2.80.931945"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1939\xe2\x80\x931945">1939\xe2\x80\x931945</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Royal_Navy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: 1939\xe2\x80\x931945">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Naval_history_of_World_War_II" title="Naval history of World War II">Naval history of World War II</a></div>\n<p>At the start of <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> in 1939, the Royal Navy was the largest in the world, with over 1,400 vessels, including:<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul><li>7 aircraft carriers \xe2\x80\x93 with 5 more under construction</li>\n<li>15 battleships and battlecruisers \xe2\x80\x93 with 5 more under construction</li>\n<li>66 cruisers \xe2\x80\x93 with 23 more under construction</li>\n<li>184 destroyers \xe2\x80\x93 with 52 under construction</li>\n<li>45 escort and patrol vessels \xe2\x80\x93 with 9 under construction and one on order</li>\n<li>60 submarines \xe2\x80\x93 with 9 under construction</li></ul>\n<p>During one of the earliest phases of the War the Royal Navy provided critical cover during <a href="/wiki/Operation_Dynamo" class="mw-redirect" title="Operation Dynamo">Operation Dynamo</a>, the British evacuations from <a href="/wiki/Dunkirk" title="Dunkirk">Dunkirk</a>. At <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Taranto" title="Battle of Taranto">Taranto</a>, <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Browne_Cunningham,_1st_Viscount_Cunningham_of_Hyndhope" class="mw-redirect" title="Andrew Browne Cunningham, 1st Viscount Cunningham of Hyndhope">Admiral Cunningham</a> commanded a fleet that launched the first all-aircraft naval attack in history. Cunningham was determined that the Navy be perceived as the United Kingdom\'s most daring military force: when warned of risks to his vessels during the Allied evacuation after the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Crete" title="Battle of Crete">Battle of Crete</a> he said, "It takes the Navy three years to build a new ship. It will take three hundred years to build a new tradition. The evacuation will continue."<sup id="cite_ref-Churchill265_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Churchill265-114">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:British_Battlecruiser_HMS_Hood_circa_1932.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/British_Battlecruiser_HMS_Hood_circa_1932.jpg/220px-British_Battlecruiser_HMS_Hood_circa_1932.jpg" width="220" height="136" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/British_Battlecruiser_HMS_Hood_circa_1932.jpg/330px-British_Battlecruiser_HMS_Hood_circa_1932.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/British_Battlecruiser_HMS_Hood_circa_1932.jpg/440px-British_Battlecruiser_HMS_Hood_circa_1932.jpg 2x" data-file-width="740" data-file-height="456" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:British_Battlecruiser_HMS_Hood_circa_1932.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>British battlecruiser <a href="/wiki/HMS_Hood_(51)" class="mw-redirect" title="HMS Hood (51)">HMS&#160;<i>Hood</i></a></div></div></div>\n<p>The Royal Navy suffered heavy <a href="/wiki/List_of_Royal_Navy_losses_in_World_War_II" title="List of Royal Navy losses in World War II">losses</a> in the first two years of the war, including the carriers <a href="/wiki/HMS_Courageous_(50)" title="HMS Courageous (50)"><i>Courageous</i></a>, <a href="/wiki/HMS_Glorious" title="HMS Glorious"><i>Glorious</i></a> and <a href="/wiki/HMS_Ark_Royal_(91)" title="HMS Ark Royal (91)"><i>Ark Royal</i></a>, the battleships <a href="/wiki/HMS_Royal_Oak_(08)" title="HMS Royal Oak (08)"><i>Royal Oak</i></a> and <a href="/wiki/HMS_Barham_(04)" title="HMS Barham (04)"><i>Barham</i></a> and the battlecruiser <a href="/wiki/HMS_Hood_(51)" class="mw-redirect" title="HMS Hood (51)"><i>Hood</i></a> in the <a href="/wiki/European_Theatre_of_World_War_II" class="mw-redirect" title="European Theatre of World War II">European Theatre</a>, and the carrier <a href="/wiki/HMS_Hermes_(95)" title="HMS Hermes (95)"><i>Hermes</i></a>, the battleship <a href="/wiki/HMS_Prince_of_Wales_(53)" title="HMS Prince of Wales (53)"><i>Prince of Wales</i></a>, the battlecruiser <a href="/wiki/HMS_Repulse_(1916)" title="HMS Repulse (1916)"><i>Repulse</i></a> and the <a href="/wiki/Heavy_cruisers" class="mw-redirect" title="Heavy cruisers">heavy cruisers</a> <a href="/wiki/HMS_Exeter_(68)" title="HMS Exeter (68)"><i>Exeter</i></a>, <a href="/wiki/HMS_Dorsetshire_(40)" title="HMS Dorsetshire (40)"><i>Dorsetshire</i></a> and <a href="/wiki/HMS_Cornwall_(56)" title="HMS Cornwall (56)"><i>Cornwall</i></a> in the Asian Theatre. Of the 1,418 men aboard <i>Hood</i>, only three survived its sinking.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> Over 3,000 people were lost when the converted <a href="/wiki/Troopship" title="Troopship">troopship</a> <i><a href="/wiki/RMS_Lancastria" title="RMS Lancastria">Lancastria</a></i> was sunk in June 1940, the greatest maritime disaster in Britain\'s history.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> There were however also successes against enemy surface ships, as in the battles of <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_River_Plate" title="Battle of the River Plate">the River Plate</a> in 1939, <a href="/wiki/Battles_of_Narvik" title="Battles of Narvik">Narvik</a> in 1940 and <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Cape_Matapan" title="Battle of Cape Matapan">Cape Matapan</a> in 1941, and the sinking of the German capital ships <i><a href="/wiki/German_battleship_Bismarck" title="German battleship Bismarck">Bismarck</a></i> in 1941 and <i><a href="/wiki/German_battleship_Scharnhorst" title="German battleship Scharnhorst">Scharnhorst</a></i> in 1943.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Navy\'s most critical struggle was the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Atlantic" title="Battle of the Atlantic">Battle of the Atlantic</a> defending Britain\'s vital commercial supply lines against U-boat attack. A traditional convoy system was instituted from the start of the war, but German submarine tactics, based on group attacks by "wolf-packs", were much more effective than in the previous war, and the threat remained serious for well over three years. Defences were strengthened by deployment of purpose-built escorts, of <a href="/wiki/Escort_carriers" class="mw-redirect" title="Escort carriers">escort carriers</a>, of long-range patrol aircraft, improved anti-submarine weapons and sensors, and by the deciphering of German signals by the code-breakers of <a href="/wiki/Bletchley_Park" title="Bletchley Park">Bletchley Park</a>. The threat was at last effectively broken by devastating losses inflicted on the U-boats in the spring of 1943. Intense convoy battles of a different sort, against combined air, surface and submarine threats, were fought off enemy-controlled coasts in <a href="/wiki/Arctic_convoys_of_World_War_II" title="Arctic convoys of World War II">the Arctic</a>, where Britain ran supply convoys through to Russia, and in the Mediterranean, where the struggle focused on <a href="/wiki/Malta_convoys" title="Malta convoys">Convoys to Malta</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Navy was also vital in guarding the sea lanes that enabled British forces to fight in North Africa, the Mediterranean and the Far East. Naval supremacy was essential to amphibious operations such as the invasions of <a href="/wiki/Operation_Torch" title="Operation Torch">Northwest Africa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Operation_Husky" class="mw-redirect" title="Operation Husky">Sicily</a>, <a href="/wiki/Allied_invasion_of_Italy" title="Allied invasion of Italy">Italy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Operation_Overlord" title="Operation Overlord">Normandy</a>. By the end of the war the Royal Navy comprised over 4,800 ships, and was the second largest fleet in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Postwar_period_and_early_21st_century">Postwar period and early 21st century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Royal_Navy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Postwar period and early 21st century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Structure_of_the_Royal_Navy_in_1989" title="Structure of the Royal Navy in 1989">Structure of the Royal Navy in 1989</a></div>\n<p>After the Second World War, the decline of the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a> and the economic hardships in Britain forced the reduction in the size and capability of the Royal Navy. All of the pre-war ships (except for the <a href="/wiki/Town_class_cruiser_(1936)" class="mw-redirect" title="Town class cruiser (1936)">Town-class</a> light cruisers) were quickly retired and most sold for scrapping over the years 1945\xe2\x80\x9348, and only the best condition ships (the four surviving KG-V class battleships, carriers, cruisers, and some destroyers) were retained and refitted for service. The increasingly powerful United States Navy took on the former role of the Royal Navy as global naval power and police force of the sea. The combination of the threat of the Soviet Union, and Britain\'s commitments throughout the world, created a new role for the Navy. Governments since the Second World War have had to balance commitments with increasing budgetary pressures, partly due to the increasing cost of weapons systems, what historian <a href="/wiki/Paul_Kennedy" title="Paul Kennedy">Paul Kennedy</a> called the <a href="/wiki/Upward_Spiral" title="Upward Spiral">Upward Spiral</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>These pressures were exacerbated by bitter inter-service rivalry. A modest new construction programme was initiated with some new carriers (<a href="/wiki/1942_Design_Light_Fleet_Carrier" title="1942 Design Light Fleet Carrier"><i>Majestic</i>-</a> and <a href="/wiki/Centaur-class_aircraft_carrier" title="Centaur-class aircraft carrier"><i>Centaur</i>-class</a> light carriers, and <a href="/wiki/Audacious-class_aircraft_carrier" title="Audacious-class aircraft carrier"><i>Audacious</i>-class</a> large carriers, such as <a href="/wiki/HMS_Ark_Royal_(R09)" title="HMS Ark Royal (R09)">HMS <i>Ark Royal</i></a>, being completed between 1948 through 1958), along with three <a href="/wiki/Tiger-class_cruiser" title="Tiger-class cruiser"><i>Tiger</i>-class</a> cruisers (completed 1959\xe2\x80\x9361), the <a href="/wiki/Daring-class_destroyer_(1949)" title="Daring-class destroyer (1949)"><i>Daring</i>-class destroyers</a> in the 1950s, and finally the <a href="/wiki/County-class_destroyer" title="County-class destroyer">County-class</a> guided missile destroyers completed in the 1960s.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Vanguard_at_Faslane_02.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Vanguard_at_Faslane_02.jpg/220px-Vanguard_at_Faslane_02.jpg" width="220" height="166" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Vanguard_at_Faslane_02.jpg/330px-Vanguard_at_Faslane_02.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Vanguard_at_Faslane_02.jpg/440px-Vanguard_at_Faslane_02.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2547" data-file-height="1921" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Vanguard_at_Faslane_02.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/HMS_Vanguard_(S28)" title="HMS Vanguard (S28)">HMS&#160;<i>Vanguard</i></a> of the current <a href="/wiki/Vanguard-class_submarine" title="Vanguard-class submarine"><i>Vanguard</i>-class</a> <a href="/wiki/Ballistic_missile_submarine" title="Ballistic missile submarine">ballistic missile submarines</a></div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/HMS_Dreadnought_(S101)" title="HMS Dreadnought (S101)">HMS&#160;<i>Dreadnought</i></a>, the Royal Navy\'s first <a href="/wiki/SSN_(hull_classification_symbol)" title="SSN (hull classification symbol)">nuclear submarine</a>, was launched in the 1960s. The navy also received its first nuclear weapons with the introduction of the first of the <a href="/wiki/Resolution-class_submarine" title="Resolution-class submarine"><i>Resolution</i>-class submarines</a> armed with the <a href="/wiki/Polaris_missile" class="mw-redirect" title="Polaris missile">Polaris missile</a>. The introduction of Polaris followed the cancellation of the <a href="/wiki/GAM-87_Skybolt" title="GAM-87 Skybolt">GAM-87 Skybolt</a> missile which had been proposed for use by the Air Force\'s <a href="/wiki/V_bomber" title="V bomber">V bomber</a> force. By the 1990s, the navy became responsible for the maintenance of the UK\'s entire <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapons_and_the_United_Kingdom" title="Nuclear weapons and the United Kingdom">nuclear arsenal</a>. The financial costs attached to nuclear deterrence became an increasingly significant issue for the navy.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:HMS_Illustrious_at_Speed_MOD_45155641.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/HMS_Illustrious_at_Speed_MOD_45155641.jpg/170px-HMS_Illustrious_at_Speed_MOD_45155641.jpg" width="170" height="212" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/HMS_Illustrious_at_Speed_MOD_45155641.jpg/255px-HMS_Illustrious_at_Speed_MOD_45155641.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/HMS_Illustrious_at_Speed_MOD_45155641.jpg/340px-HMS_Illustrious_at_Speed_MOD_45155641.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1979" data-file-height="2473" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:HMS_Illustrious_at_Speed_MOD_45155641.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/HMS_Illustrious_(R06)" title="HMS Illustrious (R06)">HMS&#160;<i>Illustrious</i></a>, an <a href="/wiki/Invincible-class_aircraft_carrier" title="Invincible-class aircraft carrier">Invincible-class</a> <a href="/wiki/Aircraft_carrier" title="Aircraft carrier">aircraft carrier</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The Navy began plans to replace its fleet of aircraft carriers in the mid-1960s. A plan was drawn up for three large aircraft carriers, each displacing about 60,000&#160;tons; the plan was designated <a href="/wiki/CVA-01" title="CVA-01">CVA-01</a>. These carriers would be able to operate the latest aircraft coming into service and keep the Royal Navy\'s place as a major naval power. The new <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)" title="Labour Party (UK)">Labour</a> government that came to power in 1964 was determined to cut defence expenditure as a means to reduce public spending, and in the <a href="/wiki/1966_Defence_White_Paper" title="1966 Defence White Paper">1966 Defence White Paper</a> the project was cancelled.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> The existing carriers (all built during, or just after World War II) were refitted, two (<a href="/wiki/HMS_Bulwark_(R08)" title="HMS Bulwark (R08)"><i>Bulwark</i></a> and <a href="/wiki/HMS_Albion_(R07)" title="HMS Albion (R07)"><i>Albion</i></a>) becoming <i>commando</i> carriers, and four (<a href="/wiki/HMS_Victorious_(R38)" title="HMS Victorious (R38)"><i>Victorious</i></a>, <a href="/wiki/HMS_Eagle_(R05)" title="HMS Eagle (R05)"><i>Eagle</i></a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Hermes" title="Hermes">Hermes</a></i>, and <a href="/wiki/HMS_Ark_Royal_(R09)" title="HMS Ark Royal (R09)"><i>Ark Royal</i></a>) being completed or rebuilt. Starting in 1965 with <a href="/wiki/HMS_Centaur_(R06)" title="HMS Centaur (R06)"><i>Centaur</i></a>, one by one these carriers were decommissioned without replacement, culminating with the 1979 retirement of <i>Ark Royal</i>. By the early 1980s, only <a href="/wiki/HMS_Hermes_(R12)" title="HMS Hermes (R12)"><i>Hermes</i></a> survived and received a refit (just in time for the <a href="/wiki/Falklands_War" title="Falklands War">Falklands War</a>), to operate <a href="/wiki/Sea_Harriers" class="mw-redirect" title="Sea Harriers">Sea Harriers</a>. She operated along with three much smaller <a href="/wiki/Invincible-class_aircraft_carrier" title="Invincible-class aircraft carrier"><i>Invincible</i>-class</a> aircraft carriers, and the fleet was now centred around anti-submarine warfare in the north Atlantic as opposed to its former position with worldwide strike capability. Along with the war era carriers, all of the war built cruisers and destroyers, along with the post-war built <i>Tiger</i>-class cruisers and large <a href="/wiki/County-class_destroyer" title="County-class destroyer">County-class</a> guided missile destroyers were either retired or sold by 1984.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>One of the most important operations conducted predominantly by the Royal Navy after the Second World War was the 1982 defeat of Argentina in the <a href="/wiki/Falklands_War" title="Falklands War">Falkland Islands War</a>. Despite losing four naval ships and other civilian and <a href="/wiki/Royal_Fleet_Auxiliary" title="Royal Fleet Auxiliary">RFA</a> ships, the Royal Navy fought and won a war over 8,000&#160;miles (12,000&#160;km) from Great Britain. <a href="/wiki/HMS_Conqueror_(S48)" title="HMS Conqueror (S48)">HMS&#160;<i>Conqueror</i></a> is the only nuclear-powered submarine to have engaged an enemy ship with torpedoes, sinking the cruiser <a href="/wiki/ARA_General_Belgrano" title="ARA General Belgrano">ARA <i>General Belgrano</i></a>.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:HMS_Albion_MOD_45151289.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/HMS_Albion_MOD_45151289.jpg/220px-HMS_Albion_MOD_45151289.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/HMS_Albion_MOD_45151289.jpg/330px-HMS_Albion_MOD_45151289.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/HMS_Albion_MOD_45151289.jpg/440px-HMS_Albion_MOD_45151289.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3600" data-file-height="2400" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:HMS_Albion_MOD_45151289.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/HMS_Albion_(L14)" title="HMS Albion (L14)">HMS&#160;<i>Albion</i></a>, an <a href="/wiki/Albion-class_landing_platform_dock" title="Albion-class landing platform dock"><i>Albion</i>-class landing platform dock</a> on exercise with the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands_Marine_Corps" title="Netherlands Marine Corps">Netherlands Marine Corps</a> in 2008</div></div></div>\n<p>Before the Falklands War, Defence Secretary <a href="/wiki/John_Nott" title="John Nott">John Nott</a> had advocated and initiated a series of cutbacks to the Navy.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> The Falklands War though, provided a reprieve in Nott-proposed cutbacks, and proved a need for the Royal Navy to regain an expeditionary and <a href="/wiki/Littoral" class="mw-redirect" title="Littoral">littoral</a> capability which, with its resources and structure at the time, would prove difficult. At the beginning of the 1980s, the Royal Navy was a force focused on blue-water anti-submarine warfare. Its purpose was to search for and destroy Soviet <a href="/wiki/Submarines" class="mw-redirect" title="Submarines">submarines</a> in the North <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_(ocean)" class="mw-redirect" title="Atlantic (ocean)">Atlantic</a>, and to operate the nuclear deterrent submarine force. For a time <i>Hermes</i> was retained, along with all three of the <i>Invincible</i>-class light aircraft carriers. More <a href="/wiki/Sea_Harriers" class="mw-redirect" title="Sea Harriers">Sea Harriers</a> were ordered; not just to replace losses, but to also increase the size of the <a href="/wiki/Fleet_Air_Arm" title="Fleet Air Arm">Fleet Air Arm</a>. New and more capable ships were built; notably the <a href="/wiki/Type_42_destroyer" title="Type 42 destroyer"><i>Sheffield</i>-class destroyers</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Type_21_frigate" title="Type 21 frigate">Type 21</a>, <a href="/wiki/Type_22_frigate" title="Type 22 frigate">Type 22</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Type_23_frigate" title="Type 23 frigate">Type 23</a> <a href="/wiki/Frigate" title="Frigate">frigates</a>, new LPDs of the <a href="/wiki/Albion-class_landing_platform_dock" title="Albion-class landing platform dock"><i>Albion</i> class</a>, and <a href="/wiki/HMS_Ocean_(L12)" title="HMS Ocean (L12)">HMS&#160;<i>Ocean</i></a>, but never in the numbers of the ships that they replaced. As a result, the Royal Navy surface fleet continues to reduce in size. A 2013 report found that the current RN was already too small, and that Britain would have to depend on her allies if her territories were attacked.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Royal Navy also took part in the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_War" title="Gulf War">Gulf War</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Kosovo_conflict" class="mw-redirect" title="Kosovo conflict">Kosovo conflict</a>, the <a href="/wiki/War_in_Afghanistan_(2001%E2%80%93present)" title="War in Afghanistan (2001\xe2\x80\x93present)">Afghanistan Campaign</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/2003_invasion_of_Iraq" title="2003 invasion of Iraq">2003 invasion of Iraq</a>, the last of which saw RN warships <a href="/wiki/Naval_Gunfire_Support" class="mw-redirect" title="Naval Gunfire Support">bombard</a> positions in support of the <a href="/wiki/Al_Faw_Peninsula" class="mw-redirect" title="Al Faw Peninsula">Al Faw Peninsula</a> landings by <a href="/wiki/Royal_Marines" title="Royal Marines">Royal Marines</a>. In August 2005, the Royal Navy rescued seven Russians stranded in a submarine off the <a href="/wiki/Kamchatka_peninsula" class="mw-redirect" title="Kamchatka peninsula">Kamchatka peninsula</a>. The Navy\'s <a href="/wiki/Scorpio_45" class="mw-redirect" title="Scorpio 45">Scorpio 45</a> remote-controlled mini-sub freed the Russian submarine from the fishing nets and cables that had held it for three days. The Royal Navy was also involved in an <a href="/wiki/Action_of_11_November_2008" title="Action of 11 November 2008">incident involving Somali pirates</a> in November 2008, after the pirates tried to capture a civilian vessel.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The global economic recession of 2008 had a significant impact on the Royal Navy resulting in the <b>Strategic Defence &amp; Security Review 2010</b> which made sweeping cuts to the Navy\'s budget. The <i>Harrier</i> aircraft were retired with some being presented to museums and the rest being sold to the United States for spare parts to keep their aircraft flying. The carrier <i>Ark Royal</i> and the remaining Type-22 frigates were all removed from service and sold for scrap.  <i><a href="/wiki/HMS_Illustrious_(R06)" title="HMS Illustrious (R06)">HMS Illustrious</a></i> however, was retained through to 2014 in the <a href="/wiki/Landing_Platform_Helicopter" title="Landing Platform Helicopter">LPH</a> role, until <i><a href="/wiki/HMS_Ocean_(L12)" title="HMS Ocean (L12)">HMS Ocean</a></i> completed her refit.  Plans were made to allow <i>Illustrious</i> to be retained as a floating museum, but by summer of 2016 she too was sold for scrap.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> The future of <i>Albion</i> and <i>Bulwark</i> is uncertain as funds may not be available to allow them to remain in service.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> The Royal Navy was to receive 12 <a href="/wiki/Type_45_destroyer" title="Type 45 destroyer">Type 45 destroyers</a> as a replacement for the older Type 42 class that was completely retired by 2013. The number was later reduced to 6 vessels, all in service by 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2015, the Royal Navy was deployed to the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean</a> in the <a href="/wiki/EU_Navfor_Med" class="mw-redirect" title="EU Navfor Med">mission</a> to rescue <a href="/wiki/European_migration_crisis" class="mw-redirect" title="European migration crisis">migrants crossing the Mediterranean</a> from <a href="/wiki/Libya" title="Libya">Libya</a> to <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> By spring 2018, the Royal Navy had decommissioned HMS <i>Ocean</i>, as well as started the replacement of the River class Offshore Patrol Vessels. The first of the new <a href="/wiki/Queen_Elizabeth-class_aircraft_carrier" title="Queen Elizabeth-class aircraft carrier"><i>Queen Elizabeth</i>-class carriers</a> was undergoing tests and workups before her first fixed-wing aircraft arrive later in the year, and design work was underway for the new generation of nuclear deterrent submarines.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> By July 2017 the first of 8 new frigates was laid down, the <a href="/wiki/Type_26_frigate" title="Type 26 frigate">Type 26 frigate</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> There is also plans to build up to 10 Global Combat Ships to replace some of the older frigates in the fleet.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Royal_Navy_today">Royal Navy today</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Royal_Navy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Royal Navy today">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Personnel">Personnel</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Royal_Navy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Personnel">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:BRNC-Dartmouth.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/BRNC-Dartmouth.jpg/220px-BRNC-Dartmouth.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/BRNC-Dartmouth.jpg/330px-BRNC-Dartmouth.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/BRNC-Dartmouth.jpg/440px-BRNC-Dartmouth.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1200" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:BRNC-Dartmouth.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Britannia Royal Naval College</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/HMS_Raleigh_(shore_establishment)" title="HMS Raleigh (shore establishment)">HMS&#160;<i>Raleigh</i></a> at <a href="/wiki/Torpoint,_Cornwall" class="mw-redirect" title="Torpoint, Cornwall">Torpoint, Cornwall</a>, is the basic training facility for newly enlisted personnel. <a href="/wiki/Britannia_Royal_Naval_College" title="Britannia Royal Naval College">Britannia Royal Naval College</a> is the initial officer training establishment for the navy, located at <a href="/wiki/Dartmouth,_Devon" title="Dartmouth, Devon">Dartmouth, Devon</a>. Personnel are divided into a general duties branch, which includes those seamen officers eligible for command, and other branches including the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Naval_Engineers" title="Royal Naval Engineers">Royal Naval Engineers</a>, medical, and <a href="/wiki/Logistics_Officer" title="Logistics Officer">Logistics Officers</a>, the renamed <a href="/wiki/Supply_Officer" class="mw-redirect" title="Supply Officer">Supply Officer</a> branch. Present day officers and ratings have several different <a href="/wiki/Royal_Navy_uniform" class="mw-redirect" title="Royal Navy uniform">Royal Navy uniforms</a>; some are blue, others are white. Women began to join the Royal Navy in 1917 with the formation of the <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_Royal_Naval_Service" title="Women&#39;s Royal Naval Service">Women\'s Royal Naval Service</a> (WRNS), which was disbanded after the end of the First World War in 1919. It was revived in 1939, and the WRNS continued until disbandment in 1993, as a result of the decision to fully integrate women into the structures of the Royal Navy. Women now serve in all sections of the Royal Navy including the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Marines" title="Royal Marines">Royal Marines</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>By January 2015, the Naval Service (Royal Navy and Royal Marines) numbered some 32,880 Regular<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> and 3,040 <a href="/wiki/Maritime_Reserve_(United_Kingdom)" title="Maritime Reserve (United Kingdom)">Maritime Reserve</a> personnel (<a href="/wiki/Royal_Naval_Reserve" title="Royal Naval Reserve">Royal Naval Reserve</a> and <a href="/wiki/Royal_Marines_Reserve" title="Royal Marines Reserve">Royal Marines Reserve</a>),<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> giving a combined component strength of 35,920 personnel. In addition to the active elements of the Naval Service (Regular and Maritime Reserve), all ex-Regular personnel remain liable to be recalled for duty in <a href="/wiki/Total_war" title="Total war">a time of need</a>, this is known as the <a href="/wiki/Regular_Reserve_(United_Kingdom)" title="Regular Reserve (United Kingdom)">Regular Reserve</a>. In 2002, there were 26,520 Regular Reserves of the Naval Service, of which 13,720 served in the <a href="/wiki/Regular_Reserve_(United_Kingdom)#Royal_Fleet_Reserve" title="Regular Reserve (United Kingdom)">Royal Fleet Reserve</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> Publications since April 2013 no-longer report the entire strength of the Regular Reserve, instead they only give a figure for Regular Reserves who serve in the Royal Fleet Reserve.<sup id="cite_ref-DASA-Reserves-2013_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DASA-Reserves-2013-140">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> They had a strength of 7,960 personnel in 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-DASA-Reserves-2014_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DASA-Reserves-2014-141">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Surface_fleet">Surface fleet</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Royal_Navy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Surface fleet">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_active_Royal_Navy_ships" title="List of active Royal Navy ships">List of active Royal Navy ships</a></div> <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Royal_Navy_Surface_Fleet" title="Royal Navy Surface Fleet">Royal Navy Surface Fleet</a></div>\n<dl><dt>Large fleet units&#160;\xe2\x80\x93 amphibious and carriers</dt></dl>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:HMS_Queen_Elizabeth_(R08)_underway_during_trials_with_HMS_Sutherland_(F81)_and_HMS_Iron_Duke_(F234)_on_28_June_2017_(45162784).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/HMS_Queen_Elizabeth_%28R08%29_underway_during_trials_with_HMS_Sutherland_%28F81%29_and_HMS_Iron_Duke_%28F234%29_on_28_June_2017_%2845162784%29.jpg/220px-HMS_Queen_Elizabeth_%28R08%29_underway_during_trials_with_HMS_Sutherland_%28F81%29_and_HMS_Iron_Duke_%28F234%29_on_28_June_2017_%2845162784%29.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/HMS_Queen_Elizabeth_%28R08%29_underway_during_trials_with_HMS_Sutherland_%28F81%29_and_HMS_Iron_Duke_%28F234%29_on_28_June_2017_%2845162784%29.jpg/330px-HMS_Queen_Elizabeth_%28R08%29_underway_during_trials_with_HMS_Sutherland_%28F81%29_and_HMS_Iron_Duke_%28F234%29_on_28_June_2017_%2845162784%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/HMS_Queen_Elizabeth_%28R08%29_underway_during_trials_with_HMS_Sutherland_%28F81%29_and_HMS_Iron_Duke_%28F234%29_on_28_June_2017_%2845162784%29.jpg/440px-HMS_Queen_Elizabeth_%28R08%29_underway_during_trials_with_HMS_Sutherland_%28F81%29_and_HMS_Iron_Duke_%28F234%29_on_28_June_2017_%2845162784%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3600" data-file-height="2400" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:HMS_Queen_Elizabeth_(R08)_underway_during_trials_with_HMS_Sutherland_(F81)_and_HMS_Iron_Duke_(F234)_on_28_June_2017_(45162784).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/HMS_Queen_Elizabeth_(R08)" title="HMS Queen Elizabeth (R08)">HMS&#160;<i>Queen Elizabeth</i></a>, a <a href="/wiki/Queen_Elizabeth-class_aircraft_carrier" title="Queen Elizabeth-class aircraft carrier"><i>Queen Elizabeth</i>-class</a> <a href="/wiki/Supercarrier" class="mw-redirect" title="Supercarrier">supercarrier</a> on sea trials in June 2017.</div></div></div>\n<p>The large fleet units in the Royal Navy consisted of <a href="/wiki/Amphibious_warfare_ship" title="Amphibious warfare ship">amphibious warfare ships</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aircraft_carriers" class="mw-redirect" title="Aircraft carriers">aircraft carriers</a>, until August 2014, when the last <a href="/wiki/Invincible-class_aircraft_carrier" title="Invincible-class aircraft carrier">Invincible-class aircraft carrier</a> was decommissioned. Amphibious warfare ships in current service include two <a href="/wiki/Landing_platform_dock" class="mw-redirect" title="Landing platform dock">landing platform docks</a> (<a href="/wiki/HMS_Albion_(L14)" title="HMS Albion (L14)">HMS&#160;<i>Albion</i></a> and <a href="/wiki/HMS_Bulwark_(L15)" title="HMS Bulwark (L15)">HMS&#160;<i>Bulwark</i></a>). While their primary role is to conduct amphibious warfare, they have also been deployed for <a href="/wiki/Humanitarian_aid" title="Humanitarian aid">humanitarian aid</a> missions.<sup id="cite_ref-human_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-human-142">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/HMS_Illustrious_(R06)" title="HMS Illustrious (R06)">HMS&#160;<i>Illustrious</i></a> was the sole remaining aircraft carrier in service with the Royal Navy. Following the retirement of the <a href="/wiki/British_Aerospace_Harrier_II" title="British Aerospace Harrier II">Harrier GR9</a> aircraft in 2010, <i>Illustrious</i> had been serving as an amphibious assault ship while <i>Ocean</i> was in refit. <i>Illustrious</i> was decommissioned on 28 August 2014, after <i>Ocean</i> had returned to active duty.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> However, two much larger <a href="/wiki/Queen_Elizabeth-class_aircraft_carrier" title="Queen Elizabeth-class aircraft carrier"><i>Queen Elizabeth</i>-class aircraft carriers</a> are under construction. These carriers are expected to cost <a href="/wiki/Pound_sterling" title="Pound sterling">\xc2\xa3</a>6&#160;billion (double the original estimate) and displace 70,600&#160;tonnes.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> <i>Queen Elizabeth</i> commenced flight trials in 2018. Both are intended to operate the STOVL variant of the <a href="/wiki/F-35_Lightning_II" class="mw-redirect" title="F-35 Lightning II">F-35 Lightning II</a>. The first, <a href="/wiki/HMS_Queen_Elizabeth_(R08)" title="HMS Queen Elizabeth (R08)">HMS <i>Queen Elizabeth</i></a> began sea trials in June 2017 and will enter service in 2020, while the second, <a href="/wiki/HMS_Prince_of_Wales_(R09)" title="HMS Prince of Wales (R09)">HMS <i>Prince of Wales</i></a> will enter service in 2023.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-facts_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-facts-146">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Navy\'s large fleet units are supported by the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Fleet_Auxiliary" title="Royal Fleet Auxiliary">Royal Fleet Auxiliary</a> which possesses three <a href="/wiki/Amphibious_transport_dock" title="Amphibious transport dock">amphibious transport docks</a> within its operational craft. These are known as the <a href="/wiki/Bay-class_landing_ship" title="Bay-class landing ship"><i>Bay</i>-class</a> landing ships, of which four were introduced in 2006\xe2\x80\x932007, but one was sold to the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Australian_Navy" title="Royal Australian Navy">Royal Australian Navy</a> in 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> In November 2006, the <a href="/wiki/First_Sea_Lord" title="First Sea Lord">First Sea Lord</a> Admiral Sir <a href="/wiki/Jonathon_Band" title="Jonathon Band">Jonathon Band</a> described the Royal Fleet Auxiliary vessels as "a major uplift in the Royal Navy\'s war fighting capability."<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<dl><dt>Major surface combatants&#160;\xe2\x80\x93 destroyers and frigates</dt></dl>\n<p>The escort fleet, in the form of <a href="/wiki/Guided_missile_destroyer" title="Guided missile destroyer">guided missile destroyers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Frigates" class="mw-redirect" title="Frigates">frigates</a>, is the traditional workhorse of the Navy.<sup id="cite_ref-MODRNinfo_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MODRNinfo-150">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> As of  January 2018<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Royal_Navy&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup> there are six <a href="/wiki/Type_45_destroyer" title="Type 45 destroyer">Type 45 destroyers</a> and 13 <a href="/wiki/Type_23_frigate" title="Type 23 frigate">Type 23 frigates</a> in active service. Among their primary roles is to provide escort for the larger capital ships\xe2\x80\x94protecting them from air, surface and subsurface threats. Other duties include undertaking the Royal Navy\'s <a href="/wiki/Standing_Royal_Navy_deployments" title="Standing Royal Navy deployments">standing deployments</a> across the globe, which often consists of: counter-narcotics, anti-piracy missions and providing humanitarian aid.<sup id="cite_ref-human_142-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-human-142">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Duncan_(7899777334).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Duncan_%287899777334%29.jpg/220px-Duncan_%287899777334%29.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Duncan_%287899777334%29.jpg/330px-Duncan_%287899777334%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Duncan_%287899777334%29.jpg/440px-Duncan_%287899777334%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="682" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Duncan_(7899777334).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/HMS_Duncan_(D37)" title="HMS Duncan (D37)">HMS&#160;<i>Duncan</i></a>, the <a href="/wiki/Type_45_destroyer" title="Type 45 destroyer">Type 45</a> <a href="/wiki/Guided_missile_destroyer" title="Guided missile destroyer">guided missile destroyer</a></div></div></div>\n<p>All six Type 45 destroyers have been built and are in commission, with <a href="/wiki/HMS_Duncan_(D37)" title="HMS Duncan (D37)">HMS&#160;<i>Duncan</i></a> being the last and final Type 45 entering service in September 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> The new Type 45 destroyers replaced the older <a href="/wiki/Type_42_destroyer" title="Type 42 destroyer">Type 42 destroyers</a>. The Type 45 is primarily designed for <a href="/wiki/Anti-aircraft_warfare" title="Anti-aircraft warfare">anti-aircraft and anti-missile warfare</a> and the Royal Navy describe the destroyers mission as "to shield the Fleet from air attack".<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> They are equipped with the <a href="/wiki/PAAMS" title="PAAMS">PAAMS</a> (also known as Sea Viper) integrated anti-aircraft warfare system which incorporates the sophisticated <a href="/wiki/SAMPSON" title="SAMPSON">SAMPSON</a> and <a href="/wiki/S1850M" title="S1850M">S1850M</a> long range radars and the <a href="/wiki/Aster_(missile_family)" title="Aster (missile family)">Aster 15 and 30</a> missiles.<sup id="cite_ref-BAEType45_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BAEType45-153">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Initially, 16 Type 23 frigates were delivered to the Royal Navy, with the final vessel, <a href="/wiki/HMS_St_Albans_(F83)" title="HMS St Albans (F83)">HMS&#160;<i>St Albans</i></a>, commissioned in June 2002. However, the 2004 review of defence spending (<a href="/wiki/Delivering_Security_in_a_Changing_World" title="Delivering Security in a Changing World">Delivering Security in a Changing World</a>) announced that three frigates of the fleet of sixteen would be <a href="/wiki/Paid_off" class="mw-redirect" title="Paid off">paid off</a> as part of a continuous cost-cutting strategy, and these were subsequently sold to the <a href="/wiki/Chilean_Navy" title="Chilean Navy">Chilean Navy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-SellType23_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SellType23-154">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> The 2010 <a href="/wiki/Strategic_Defence_and_Security_Review_2010" title="Strategic Defence and Security Review 2010">Strategic Defence and Security Review</a> announced that the remaining 13 Type 23 frigates would eventually be replaced by the <a href="/wiki/Global_Combat_Ship" class="mw-redirect" title="Global Combat Ship">Global Combat Ship</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<dl><dt>Patrol and survey vessels</dt></dl>\n<p>At the beginning of the 1990s, the Royal Navy had two classes of offshore patrol vessel, the <a href="/wiki/Island-class_patrol_vessel" title="Island-class patrol vessel"><i>Island</i>-class</a>, and the larger <a href="/wiki/Castle-class_patrol_vessel" title="Castle-class patrol vessel"><i>Castle</i>-class</a>. However, in 1997, a decision was taken to replace them; this decision came in the form of three much larger offshore patrol vessels, the <a href="/wiki/River-class_patrol_vessel" title="River-class patrol vessel"><i>River</i>-class</a>. Unusually, the three River-class ships were owned by <a href="/wiki/VT_Group" title="VT Group">Vosper Thorneycroft</a>, and leased to the Royal Navy until 2013. This relationship was defined by a ground-breaking contractor logistic support contract which contracts the ships\' availability to the RN, including technical and stores support. A modified River-class vessel, <a href="/wiki/HMS_Clyde_(P257)" title="HMS Clyde (P257)">HMS&#160;<i>Clyde</i></a>, was commissioned in July 2007 and became the <a href="/wiki/Falkland_Islands" title="Falkland Islands">Falkland Islands</a> guard-ship. In November 2013, it was announced that in order to sustain shipbuilding capabilities on the Clyde, five new ocean-going patrol vessels with <a href="/wiki/AgustaWestland_AW101" title="AgustaWestland AW101">Merlin-capable</a> flightdecks would be ordered for delivery from 2017. These ships will replace the four existing River Class ships.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> In October 2014, the Ministry of Defence announced the names of the first three ships as <a href="/wiki/HMS_Forth_(P222)" title="HMS Forth (P222)">HMS <i>Forth</i></a>, HMS <i>Medway</i> and HMS <i>Trent</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> The fourth and fifth ships were ordered in December 2016, these will be named HMS <i>Spey</i> and HMS <i>Tamar</i> respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:HMS_Protector_Assisting_the_Antarctic_Community._MOD_45156397.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/HMS_Protector_Assisting_the_Antarctic_Community._MOD_45156397.jpg/220px-HMS_Protector_Assisting_the_Antarctic_Community._MOD_45156397.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/HMS_Protector_Assisting_the_Antarctic_Community._MOD_45156397.jpg/330px-HMS_Protector_Assisting_the_Antarctic_Community._MOD_45156397.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/HMS_Protector_Assisting_the_Antarctic_Community._MOD_45156397.jpg/440px-HMS_Protector_Assisting_the_Antarctic_Community._MOD_45156397.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3600" data-file-height="2400" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:HMS_Protector_Assisting_the_Antarctic_Community._MOD_45156397.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/HMS_Protector_(A173)" title="HMS Protector (A173)">HMS&#160;<i>Protector</i></a>, a Royal Navy <a href="/wiki/British_Antarctica" class="mw-redirect" title="British Antarctica">Antarctic</a> <a href="/wiki/Patrol_ship" class="mw-redirect" title="Patrol ship">patrol ship</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The Royal Navy\'s largest patrol ship is <a href="/wiki/HMS_Protector_(A173)" title="HMS Protector (A173)">HMS&#160;<i>Protector</i></a>. <i>Protector</i> is a dedicated Antarctic patrol ship that fulfils the nations mandate to provide support to the <a href="/wiki/British_Antarctic_Survey" title="British Antarctic Survey">British Antarctic Survey</a> (BAS).<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<dl><dt>Mine countermeasure</dt></dl>\n<p>Mine countermeasure vessels in service with the Royal Navy include: seven <a href="/wiki/Sandown-class_minehunter" title="Sandown-class minehunter"><i>Sandown</i>-class</a> <a href="/wiki/Minehunter" title="Minehunter">minehunters</a> and six <a href="/wiki/Hunt-class_mine_countermeasures_vessel" title="Hunt-class mine countermeasures vessel">Hunt-class</a> <a href="/wiki/Mine_countermeasures_vessel" title="Mine countermeasures vessel">mine countermeasure vessels</a>. The Hunt-class vessels combine the separate roles of the traditional <a href="/wiki/Minesweeper_(ship)" class="mw-redirect" title="Minesweeper (ship)">minesweeper</a> and the active minehunter in one hull. If required, the <i>Sandown</i> and Hunt-class vessels can take on the role of offshore patrol vessels. <a href="/wiki/HMS_Scott_(H131)" title="HMS Scott (H131)">HMS&#160;<i>Scott</i></a> is an ocean survey vessel and at 13,500 tonnes is one of the largest ships in the Navy. The other survey vessels of the Royal Navy are the two multi-role ships of the <a href="/wiki/Echo-class_survey_ship_(2002)" title="Echo-class survey ship (2002)"><i>Echo</i>-class</a>, which came into service in 2002 and 2003.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Submarine_Service">Submarine Service</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Royal_Navy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Submarine Service">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Royal_Navy_Submarine_Service" title="Royal Navy Submarine Service">Royal Navy Submarine Service</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:HMS_Astute_Arrives_at_Faslane_for_the_First_Time_MOD_45150806.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/HMS_Astute_Arrives_at_Faslane_for_the_First_Time_MOD_45150806.jpg/220px-HMS_Astute_Arrives_at_Faslane_for_the_First_Time_MOD_45150806.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/HMS_Astute_Arrives_at_Faslane_for_the_First_Time_MOD_45150806.jpg/330px-HMS_Astute_Arrives_at_Faslane_for_the_First_Time_MOD_45150806.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/HMS_Astute_Arrives_at_Faslane_for_the_First_Time_MOD_45150806.jpg/440px-HMS_Astute_Arrives_at_Faslane_for_the_First_Time_MOD_45150806.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="2000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:HMS_Astute_Arrives_at_Faslane_for_the_First_Time_MOD_45150806.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/HMS_Astute_(S119)" title="HMS Astute (S119)">HMS&#160;<i>Astute</i></a>, the first <a href="/wiki/Astute-class_submarine" title="Astute-class submarine"><i>Astute</i>-class</a> <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_submarine" title="Nuclear submarine">nuclear submarine</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The Submarine Service is the <a href="/wiki/Submarine" title="Submarine">submarine</a> based element of the Royal Navy. It is sometimes referred to as the "<i>Silent Service</i>",<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> as the submarines are generally required to operate undetected. The service was founded in 1901. The service made history in 1982 when, during the <a href="/wiki/Falklands_War" title="Falklands War">Falklands War</a>, <a href="/wiki/HMS_Conqueror_(S48)" title="HMS Conqueror (S48)">HMS&#160;<i>Conqueror</i></a> became the first nuclear-powered submarine to sink a surface ship, the <a href="/wiki/ARA_General_Belgrano" title="ARA General Belgrano">ARA <i>General Belgrano</i></a>. Today, the Submarine Service consists of <a href="/wiki/Ballistic_missile_submarine" title="Ballistic missile submarine">ballistic missile submarines</a> (SSBN) and <a href="/wiki/SSN_(hull_classification_symbol)" title="SSN (hull classification symbol)">fleet submarines</a> (SSN). All of the Royal Navy\'s submarines are <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_marine_propulsion" title="Nuclear marine propulsion">nuclear-powered</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Of ballistic missile submarines, the Royal Navy operates the four <a href="/wiki/Vanguard-class_submarine" title="Vanguard-class submarine"><i>Vanguard</i>-class</a>, each displacing nearly 16,000 tonnes and equipped with <a href="/wiki/Trident_Missile" class="mw-redirect" title="Trident Missile">Trident II</a> missiles (armed with <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapons_and_the_United_Kingdom" title="Nuclear weapons and the United Kingdom">nuclear weapons</a>) and heavyweight <a href="/wiki/Spearfish_torpedo" title="Spearfish torpedo">Spearfish torpedoes</a>, with the purpose to carry out Operation Relentless, the United Kingdom\'s Continuous At Sea Deterrent (Abbreviated to CASD). In December 2006, the Government published recommendations for a new class of four ballistic missile submarines to replace the current <i>Vanguard</i>-class, starting 2024. These new <a href="/wiki/Dreadnought-class_submarine" title="Dreadnought-class submarine"><i>Dreadnought</i>-class submarines</a> will mean that the United Kingdom will maintain a nuclear ballistic missile submarine fleet and the ability to launch nuclear weapons.<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Six fleet submarines are presently in service, with three <a href="/wiki/Trafalgar-class_submarine" title="Trafalgar-class submarine"><i>Trafalgar</i>-class</a> and three <a href="/wiki/Astute-class_submarine" title="Astute-class submarine"><i>Astute</i>-class</a> (with the remainder in construction) making up the total. The <i>Trafalgar</i>-class displace little over 5,300 tonnes when submerged and are armed with <a href="/wiki/Tomahawk_(missile)" title="Tomahawk (missile)">Tomahawk</a> land-attack missiles and Spearfish torpedoes. The <i>Astute</i>-class at 7,400 tonnes<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> are much larger and carry a larger number of Tomahawk missiles and Spearfish torpedoes. Four more <i>Astute</i>-class fleet submarines are expected to be commissioned and will eventually replace the remaining <i>Trafalgar</i>-class boats. <a href="/wiki/HMS_Artful_(S121)" title="HMS Artful (S121)">HMS&#160;<i>Artful</i></a> was the latest <i>Astute</i>-class boat to be commissioned.<sup id="cite_ref-Commissioning_165-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Commissioning-165">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the 2010 Strategic Defence and Security Review, the UK Government reaffirmed its intention to procure seven <i>Astute</i>-class submarines.<sup id="cite_ref-direct.gov.uk_166-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-direct.gov.uk-166">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fleet_Air_Arm">Fleet Air Arm</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Royal_Navy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Fleet Air Arm">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Fleet_Air_Arm" title="Fleet Air Arm">Fleet Air Arm</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:UK_F-35B_Lightning_II_MOD_45157752.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/UK_F-35B_Lightning_II_MOD_45157752.jpg/220px-UK_F-35B_Lightning_II_MOD_45157752.jpg" width="220" height="137" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/UK_F-35B_Lightning_II_MOD_45157752.jpg/330px-UK_F-35B_Lightning_II_MOD_45157752.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/UK_F-35B_Lightning_II_MOD_45157752.jpg/440px-UK_F-35B_Lightning_II_MOD_45157752.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3364" data-file-height="2095" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:UK_F-35B_Lightning_II_MOD_45157752.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/F-35_Lightning_II" class="mw-redirect" title="F-35 Lightning II">F-35B</a> will be operated from the <a href="/wiki/Queen_Elizabeth-class_aircraft_carrier" title="Queen Elizabeth-class aircraft carrier"><i>Queen Elizabeth</i>-class aircraft carriers</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The Fleet Air Arm (FAA) is the branch of the Royal Navy responsible for the operation of naval aircraft, it can trace its roots back to 1912 and the formation of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Flying_Corps" title="Royal Flying Corps">Royal Flying Corps</a>. The Fleet Air Arm currently operates helicopters: the <a href="/wiki/AgustaWestland_AW101" title="AgustaWestland AW101">AgustaWestland Merlin</a>, the <a href="/wiki/AgustaWestland_AW159" class="mw-redirect" title="AgustaWestland AW159">AgustaWestland Wildcat</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Westland_Sea_King" title="Westland Sea King">Westland Sea King</a>. Pilots designated for rotary wing service train at the <a href="/wiki/Defence_Helicopter_Flying_School" title="Defence Helicopter Flying School">Defence Helicopter Flying School</a> at RAF Shawbury.<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>With the retirement of the <a href="/wiki/Joint_Force_Harrier" title="Joint Force Harrier">Joint Force Harrier</a> and the <a href="/wiki/BAE_Harrier_II" class="mw-redirect" title="BAE Harrier II">Harrier GR7/GR9</a> strike aircraft in 2010, the FAA has no fixed-wing aircraft in front-line operations. There is an intention to operate the <a href="/wiki/Lockheed_Martin_F-35_Lightning_II" title="Lockheed Martin F-35 Lightning II">Lockheed Martin F-35 Lightning II</a> B version with the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Air_Force" title="Royal Air Force">Royal Air Force</a>. <a href="/wiki/809_Naval_Air_Squadron" title="809 Naval Air Squadron">809 NAS</a> is hoped to be the first FAA Squadron to operate that aircraft.<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup> The F-35B will be operated from the Navy\'s new <a href="/wiki/Queen_Elizabeth-class_aircraft_carrier" title="Queen Elizabeth-class aircraft carrier"><i>Queen Elizabeth</i>-class aircraft carriers</a> starting 2018 for initial flight training.<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Royal_Marines">Royal Marines</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Royal_Navy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Royal Marines">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Royal_Marines" title="Royal Marines">Royal Marines</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Royal_Marines_in_Sangin_MOD_45151554.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Royal_Marines_in_Sangin_MOD_45151554.jpg/220px-Royal_Marines_in_Sangin_MOD_45151554.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Royal_Marines_in_Sangin_MOD_45151554.jpg/330px-Royal_Marines_in_Sangin_MOD_45151554.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Royal_Marines_in_Sangin_MOD_45151554.jpg/440px-Royal_Marines_in_Sangin_MOD_45151554.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="2000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Royal_Marines_in_Sangin_MOD_45151554.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Royal_Marines" title="Royal Marines">Royal Marines</a> in Sangin, 2010</div></div></div>\n<p>The Royal Marines are an amphibious, specialised <a href="/wiki/Light_infantry" title="Light infantry">light infantry</a> force of <a href="/wiki/Commandos" class="mw-redirect" title="Commandos">commandos</a>, capable of deploying at short notice in support of <a href="/wiki/Her_Majesty%27s_Government" class="mw-redirect" title="Her Majesty&#39;s Government">Her Majesty\'s Government</a>\'s military and diplomatic objectives overseas.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup> The Royal Marines are organised into a highly mobile light infantry brigade (<a href="/wiki/3_Commando_Brigade" title="3 Commando Brigade">3 Commando Brigade</a>) and a number of separate units, including <a href="/wiki/1_Assault_Group_Royal_Marines" title="1 Assault Group Royal Marines">1 Assault Group Royal Marines</a>, <a href="/wiki/43_Commando_Fleet_Protection_Group_Royal_Marines" title="43 Commando Fleet Protection Group Royal Marines">43 Commando Fleet Protection Group Royal Marines</a> and a company strength commitment to the <a href="/wiki/Special_Forces_Support_Group" title="Special Forces Support Group">Special Forces Support Group</a>. The Corps operates in all environments and climates, though particular expertise and training is spent on <a href="/wiki/Amphibious_warfare" title="Amphibious warfare">amphibious warfare</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arctic_warfare" class="mw-redirect" title="Arctic warfare">Arctic warfare</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mountain_warfare" title="Mountain warfare">mountain warfare</a>, <a href="/wiki/Expeditionary_warfare" title="Expeditionary warfare">expeditionary warfare</a> and commitment to the UK\'s <a href="/wiki/Rapid_Reaction_Force" class="mw-redirect" title="Rapid Reaction Force">Rapid Reaction</a> Force. The Royal Marines are also the primary source of personnel for the <a href="/wiki/Special_Boat_Service" title="Special Boat Service">Special Boat Service</a> (SBS), the Royal Navy\'s contribution to the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_Special_Forces" title="United Kingdom Special Forces">United Kingdom Special Forces</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Royal Marines have seen action in a number of wars, often fighting beside the British Army; including in the Seven Years\' War, the Napoleonic Wars, the <a href="/wiki/Crimean_War" title="Crimean War">Crimean War</a>, World War I and World War II. In recent times, the Corps has been deployed in expeditionary warfare roles, such as the <a href="/wiki/Falklands_War" title="Falklands War">Falklands War</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_War" title="Gulf War">Gulf War</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Bosnian_War" title="Bosnian War">Bosnian War</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Kosovo_War" title="Kosovo War">Kosovo War</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Sierra_Leone_Civil_War" title="Sierra Leone Civil War">Sierra Leone Civil War</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Iraq_War" title="Iraq War">Iraq War</a> and the <a href="/wiki/War_in_Afghanistan_(2001%E2%80%93present)" title="War in Afghanistan (2001\xe2\x80\x93present)">War in Afghanistan</a>. The Royal Marines have international ties with allied marine forces, particularly the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Marine_Corps" title="United States Marine Corps">United States Marine Corps</a><sup id="cite_ref-mod.uk_172-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mod.uk-172">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands_Marine_Corps" title="Netherlands Marine Corps">Netherlands Marine Corps</a>/Korps Mariniers.<sup id="cite_ref-royalnavy.mod.uk_173-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-royalnavy.mod.uk-173">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Naval_bases">Naval bases</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Royal_Navy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Naval bases">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:HMS_Albion_2006.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/HMS_Albion_2006.jpg/220px-HMS_Albion_2006.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/HMS_Albion_2006.jpg/330px-HMS_Albion_2006.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/HMS_Albion_2006.jpg/440px-HMS_Albion_2006.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1840" data-file-height="1232" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:HMS_Albion_2006.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/HMS_Albion_(L14)" title="HMS Albion (L14)">HMS Albion</a> during the <i>HMNB Devonport Navy days 2006</i></div></div></div>\n<p>The Royal Navy currently uses three major naval bases in the UK, each housing its own flotilla of ships and boats ready for service:\n</p>\n<ul><li>HMNB Devonport (HMS Drake) \xe2\x80\x93 Currently the largest operational naval base in Western Europe, Devonport\'s flotilla consists of the RN\'s two amphibious assault vessels (HM Ships <i>Albion</i> and <i>Bulwark</i>), and half the fleet of <a href="/wiki/Type_23_frigate" title="Type 23 frigate">Type 23 frigates</a>. Also, Devonport homes some of the RN\'s Submarines service, including the fleet of <a href="/wiki/Trafalgar-class_submarine" title="Trafalgar-class submarine">Trafalgar Class Submarines</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>HMNB Portsmouth (HMS Nelson) \xe2\x80\x93 Home to the future Queen Elizabeth Class supercarriers, Portsmouth is also the home to the Daring Class Stealth Destroyers (commonly known as Type 45) as well as mine-clearance vessels, and a moderate fleet of Type 23 frigates as well as Fishery Protection Squadrons.<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>HMNB Clyde (HMS Neptune Or Simply \'Faslane\') \xe2\x80\x93 Situated in Central Scotland along the Clyde River, Faslane is known as the home of the UK\'s nuclear deterrent, as it maintains the fleet of Vanguard Class Ballistic Missile Submarines, as well as the fleet of Astute Class Fleet (Hunter-Killer) submarines. By 2020, Faslane will become the home to all Royal Navy submarines, and thus the RN Submarine Service. As a result, 43 Commando (Fleet Protection Group) are stationed in Faslane alongside to guard the base as well as The Royal Naval Armaments Depot at Coulport. Moreover, Faslane also operates a medium-sized fleet of patrol vessels.<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:HMS_Vigilant_alongside_Faslane_Naval_Base._MOD_45147682.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/HMS_Vigilant_alongside_Faslane_Naval_Base._MOD_45147682.jpg/220px-HMS_Vigilant_alongside_Faslane_Naval_Base._MOD_45147682.jpg" width="220" height="173" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/HMS_Vigilant_alongside_Faslane_Naval_Base._MOD_45147682.jpg/330px-HMS_Vigilant_alongside_Faslane_Naval_Base._MOD_45147682.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/HMS_Vigilant_alongside_Faslane_Naval_Base._MOD_45147682.jpg/440px-HMS_Vigilant_alongside_Faslane_Naval_Base._MOD_45147682.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="2357" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:HMS_Vigilant_alongside_Faslane_Naval_Base._MOD_45147682.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>HMS Vigilant alongside Faslane Naval Base</div></div></div> \n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Uknavy_agustawestland_eh-101_merlin_hm1_arp.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Uknavy_agustawestland_eh-101_merlin_hm1_arp.jpg/220px-Uknavy_agustawestland_eh-101_merlin_hm1_arp.jpg" width="220" height="144"
